Modelling and Analysis of
Hospital Systems
Lasse Ulrich Iversen

==PAGE:3==
Summary (English)
Hospitals in the modern society are very large systems that consist of many,
but a ﬁnite amount of, resources. These resources are to be scheduled properly,
such that patients arriving at the hospital, are treated correctly and equally.
This thesis deals with the scheduling problem that arises in hospitals, by using
an analogy between the hospital and a multiprocessor.
To support the analogy, a model of the hospital, together with a simulator tool
for diﬀerent scheduling methods, has been developed. The tool is used to adjust
diﬀerent parameters in the hospital, to see the diﬀerent outcomes, that are
produced by diﬀerent scheduling methods. The outcomes are compared with
diﬀerent factors, where one being deadlines given on treatments. This factor
is very relevant for the Danish health care sector, as cancer packages has been
introduced, to ensure treatment of cancer patients before certain deadlines.
==PAGE:4==
ii
==PAGE:5==
Summary (Danish)
Hospitaler i det moderne samfund er store systemer, der består af mange, men
ikke uendelige, ressourcer. Hospitalets job er at koordinere disse ressourcer kor-
rekt, så patienter der ankommer til hospitalet, får en korrekt og fair behandling. I
denne afhandling, vil koordinering af ressourcer være omdrejningspunktet, sam-
men med analogien imellem et hospital og en multiprocessor.
Til at støtte op om analogien, er der blevet deﬁneret en model samt udviklet et
simuleringsværktøj til forskellige simuleringsmetoder. Værktøjet kan, ved hjælp
af justeringer på nogle parametre i hospitalet, komme med forskellige udfald,
for de forskellige skeduleringsmetoder. Udfaldene fra simuleringerne bliver sam-
menlignet med nogle faktorer, hvor en af faktorerne er en tidsgrænse der er givet
på behandlinger. Denne faktor er meget relevant for det danske sundhedsvæsen,
da kræftpakkerne som er blevet introduceret, skal garentere kræftpatienter en
behandling indenfor en tidsgrænse.

==PAGE:11==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Modern hospitals are very large systems that consist of a ﬁnite set of resources
such as doctors, beds, porters, medicine and even money. The biggest challenge
for the hospitals is to optimize their resources.
As the population increases
with the average lifetime for a person, the hospitals are facing more pressure
from new and existing patients, as with a higher age, the chance of getting a
disease increases. With ﬁnite resources and increasing arrival of patients, the
optimization of patient processes is ever more needed.
In this thesis, the focus lies on modelling, predicting and optimizing the general
hospital system, such that future escalation in patient arrival can be handled
optimal. The Danish health care sector has been the foundation for the thesis,
a health care sector which, like many other, uses billions of Danish kroner each
year to treat patients. Therefore, even a small optimization will help and po-
tentially save millions of Danish kroner, while still increasing the cure ability
for the sector.
To model, predict and optimize the general hospital system, certain methodolo-
gies can be used. A very interesting one is the analogy between a heterogeneous
multiprocessor system and the hospital system.
This analogy will make the
foundation for this thesis.
==PAGE:12==
2
Introduction
1.1
Introduction
The Danish health care sector consist of 5 diﬀerent regions, which are sepa-
rated geographically. While keeping the regions separated, some regions receive
more patients than others, putting more pressure on speciﬁc regions leading to
patients being moved from hospital to hospital, or even from one region to an-
other. While in some cases it makes sense to move patients, as certain parts of
the Danish health care sector are specialized in speciﬁc diseases. But it can also
become a problem for the patient, as they risk becoming more sick and having
complications in the movement [21].
With an increasing amount of patients, even more pressure arises on the regions
to perform optimal, leading to certain sectors being optimized while others might
risk being forgotten. With forgotten sectors, the whole hospital system risk not
being optimized and might even be less optimal after the optimization in speciﬁc
sections. Also with an increasing amount of patients having several diseases at
the same time, known as multimorbidity, hospitals might need to coordinate as
patients with multimorbidity tend to need treatment diﬀerent places, for their
diﬀerent diseases. The lack of coordination between hospitals and department,
often result in bad satisfaction for the patient, as the patient might repeatedly
have to be treated the same way twice, on two diﬀerent days [13][5].
Multimorbidity patients also include cancer patients. Like with cancer at a late
stage, multimorbidity often results in other diseases being worse than normally
[3]. Cancer patients introduce other optimization problems for the regions, as
cancer patients receive great attention by the Danish health care sector and the
Danish Government. Speciﬁed by the Danish Government, cancer patients must
be treated before a certain deadline, to ensure the highest survival rate of the
very often deadly cancer disease [9][10]. With certain deadlines on treatments,
even more pressure arises on the regions, as they now have to prioritize certain
patients over other patients.
When prioritizing patients over other patients, it increases the possibility that
a patient is not satisﬁed with the hospital system. This possibility increases, as
patients with deadlines skip the queue [2], making the queue longer for patients
without deadlines. Besides the bad patient satisfaction, the Danish health care
sector risk using even more money, as the extended free hospital choice allows
for patients to be treated in private hospitals [15]. This is to be done if deadlines
cannot be met by the public health sector, and this has shown to be a costly
aﬀair for the health care sector in Denmark [16].
As the health care sector in Denmark does its best to cope with the increasing
arrivals of patients, optimization is always welcomed so that the health care
==PAGE:13==
1.2 Related work
3
sector can be even more eﬃcient. The more eﬃcient the health care sector is,
the higher the patient satisfaction becomes. Hospital systems resource usage
makes foundation for a very interesting analysis and optimization. Today, the
hospitals do for example know that there exists bottlenecks for speciﬁc resources.
One example is CT- and MR-scanners, that are used in patient processes across
many specialized departments.
The Danish health care sector is in need of
doctors with specialty in radiology, to guide, execute and assess the scanning
of patients[17][23][12]. Though if the number of doctors increases, there should
also be enough scanners able to produce enough scans to occupy all the doctors.
Hence, even if this bottleneck is solved, other bottlenecks might arise in other
departments not being able to keep up with the workload.
1.2
Related work
Early work on optimization of hospital systems led to a focus on the resources,
as the hospital resources was seen more valuable than the patient [4]. Other
more current research on optimization has lead to minimizing the waiting times
for patients, such that when patients arrive at outpatient clinics, their waiting
time to be consulted is minimized [22][8]. Here, machine learning and simulated
scheduling has been the approach to optimize the hospital system. Another
relevant study optimizes the usage of resource in an operation room, to minimize
the loss of resource usage caused by poor utilization [24].
In other work, given hospital data was the foundation for a simulation and anal-
ysis in shared resource within hospital systems. The speciﬁc type of resource,
scanners, was used here as an example to analyze the number of days a pa-
tient had to wait to get an appointment for scanning [14]. Simple scheduling
methods were used to give appointments to patients, and patient arrivals were
statistically derived from the given hospital data. Another similar study used a
heuristic approach to schedule patient appointments [11]. This study focused on
the selection of the next patient for treatment, scheduling patients with diﬀerent
classes and resource allocation of limited resources.
In [4][22][8][24], speciﬁc departments or clinics have been the foundation for an
optimization, making the speciﬁc department or clinic being optimized itself
without relations to other departments. In Petersen (2017), they took a step
backwards, modelling a complete hospital but only focusing on a speciﬁc type
of resource that is shared among all departments in the hospital [14]. In Lin,
Ling and Yeung (2017), they do not focus on a complete hospital, though it
takes into consideration the process a patient goes through, such that resource
coordination must be done for the complete patient process [11].
==PAGE:14==
4
Introduction
With regards to [14] and [11], this thesis will also take the step backward by
modelling the complete hospital, but also by focusing on a complete patient pro-
cess such that all resources in the hospital must be considered and coordinated.
1.3
Motivation
As with any system that consist of ﬁnite resources, optimization is always pos-
sible unless proven otherwise.
The hospital system can save a lot of money
if it were to know where to optimize, but optimization can also give a higher
put-through of patients that are cured, which itself should be motivation for
optimizing the system. Though in order to optimize the hospital system, the
patient ﬂow must be analyzed, ﬂaws or even bottlenecks must be found and
resolved, to make sure that they do not happen again. For the whole health
care sector, this becomes a huge task, which is why some parts must be taken
little by little and parts must be explored before the whole system can be reor-
ganized. One very critical part of the health care sector is the mentioned cancer
packages, that ensures treatment before a certain deadline. This prioritization
might inﬂuence the patient satisfaction for regular patients, which should not be
the case. Here, bottlenecks can be the reason why regular patients must wait,
as queues stack up and prioritized patients jump in front.
Scheduling a hospital system can be done in many ways. One interesting method
is looking at the analogy between a hospital system and a multiprocessor system.
These two systems are very alike, as both systems have sub-parts, that perform
jobs for the entire system. A multiprocessor system can be homogeneous, where
all processors are the same such that they all can execute the same types of tasks.
The multiprocessor system can also be heterogeneous, where all processors are
diﬀerent such that they each can be optimized to do their speciﬁc types of
tasks. The latter system type, also describes the hospital system very well, as
the hospital system is divided into departments that focus on diﬀerent categories
of medical science. Besides the sub-parts, both systems rely on shared resources
between all sub-parts. The hard disk or RAM in a multiprocessors system is
often shared by all processors while medical equipment in the hospital system
is often shared between all departments. Further on, patients can be compared
with processes in the multiprocessor system, as patients need to be scheduled
in the hospital system, just as set of processes needs to be scheduled in the
multiprocessor system. Deadlines also exists in both systems, as the general
multiprocessor system has to schedule deadlines on tasks, while patients who
follow a cancer package, must be treated before a certain amount of time.
==PAGE:15==
1.4 Objectives
5
The above analogy serves as a great foundation for a study, looking into how
the two systems can be compared but also by applying scheduling methods from
the multiprocessor system to the hospital system. From a software perspective,
the analogy will be used to create a model of the hospital system such that later
simulations in scheduling and analysis can be made.
1.4
Objectives
This thesis aims to solve the following general objects:
1. Compare the hospital system with the heterogeneous multiprocessor sys-
tem.
2. Model the hospital system.
3. Apply scheduling methods from a heterogeneous multiprocessor system to
the hospital system.
4. Analyze the result.
With the above general objectives outlined, some objectives give natural sub-
objectives to achieve as well. Comparing the hospital system with the hetero-
geneous multiprocessor system also means that the hospital system needs to be
described thoroughly, such that parts of the hospital system can be compared
with certain parts of the heterogeneous multiprocessor system. The model of
the hospital system highly depends on a thorough description of the hospital,
or the model is inconsistent with a real hospital system.
To be able to apply scheduling methods from the heterogeneous multiprocessor
system to the hospital system, diﬀerent scheduling methods need to be discov-
ered and explored to see if they ﬁt with the hospital system. At last, the analysis
should include a general assessment on how the similarities between a hospital
system and a heterogeneous multiprocessor system ﬁt, but should also include
a result of simulations, of diﬀerent scheduling methods with speciﬁc ﬁnite re-
sources and patient arrivals. From here, when reading multiprocessor system,
it is refering to a heterogeneous multiprocessor system.
The functional programming language F# is to be used to deﬁne the model and
execute scheduling simulations. F# is a very type strong language, that does
not allow null values, making it a very convenient programming language for
deﬁning a strongly related model.
==PAGE:16==
6
Introduction
1.4.1
Thesis structure
The fundamentals for the multiprocessor- and hospital systems are described in
Chapter 2 which also includes a comparison between the two systems. The chap-
ter will end with a discussion on diﬀerent multiprocessor scheduling methods
that can be relevant for the hospital system.
Chapter 3 describes the design choices made for both the model but also for
the scheduling of patients. Code snippets like Listing 1.1 will be used to show
the types that describe the model. Design choices made for simulation will also
be included together with assumptions, to justify the chosen design. Function
signatures, like the one shown in Listing 1.2, will be used to described what
functions takes as parameters and returns. The latter Chapter 4 shows complete
implementations of the functions mentioned in the design while also introducing
extra important functions.

In Chapter 5, the result of running simulations will be presented together with
an analysis of how well the simulation works. This will be presented in terms
of utilization of resources but also how deadlines are met and what the conse-
quences are for patients not having deadlines.
As the last one, Chapter 6 will conclude on the objectives together with a
discussion on how well the analogy ﬁts hospital system. Besides this, a discussion
on the simulations will be carried out together with future work and natural
extensions.
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 2
Fundamentals of the
Hospital- and
Multiprocessor System
This chapter describes the fundamental parts of both the hospital- and the
multiprocessor system, which should be used for scheduling purposes. The focus
in this thesis is on the hospital system, which means that fundamentals for
the hospital system will ﬁrst be described. Afterwards, the fundamentals in
the multiprocessor system, which can be used to make a comparison with the
hospital system, will be described, together with a ﬁnal comparison between the
two systems. Scheduling principles for each system will brieﬂy be described, to
create a foundation for the later design choices.
2.1
Hospital system
The hospital system, or just hospital, is the general term for describing the entire
system. The entire system can consist of several hospital locations. In Region
Sjælland for example, the health care sector consist, among others, of several
physical hospitals that are located at diﬀerent geographical places in the region
==PAGE:18==
8
Fundamentals of the Hospital- and Multiprocessor System
[20]. The hospital system is divided into areas that each contain a speciﬁc part
of the hospital system.
2.1.1
Area
An area describes a speciﬁc part of the hospital system.
This could for ex-
ample be the the physical hospitals, but also the psychiatry section in Region
Sjælland[19]. An area is typically divided into departments that most often have
a speciﬁc medical science to focus on and specialize in.
2.1.2
Departments and sub-departments
Departments are located inside physical hospitals, where the physical hospital is
part of an area. The departments usually have a speciﬁc part of medical science
to focus on, or are organized as a service department for the rest of the physical
hospital. All physical hospitals do not have a department for each part of the
medical science. A department is often separated into diﬀerent sub-departments
that could be a ward or an outpatient clinic [14].
2.1.3
Resources
In the hospital system there are two types of resources; personal and equipment.
Each type of resource can be divided into speciﬁc resources, where an equip-
ment resource can be a bed, computer, heartbeat monitor or a CT-scanner for
example. A personal resource can be a doctor, nurse or surgeon for example,
where these can be divided even further to doctors that are radiologists or urol-
ogists. A speciﬁc personal for a CT-scanner is a radiographer. Resources are
limited in the hospital system and each have a cost, and most often a duration
for usage. Resources in the hospital can be shared or private. Shared resources
are the resources that are shared among all departments and physical hospitals,
which include beds and heartbeat monitors for example. Private resources are
resources that are only used by the speciﬁc department, such as a CT-scanner
which is used only by a radiology department. Personal resources such as a heart
surgeon can only be used by the cardiology department, while a nurse without
specialization can be used among all departments, though the nurse most often
has a speciﬁc department where he/she is hired. A porter is a personal resource
that is used among all departments, because they move patients in their beds
between departments.
==PAGE:19==
2.2 Multiprocessor system
9
2.1.4
Patient processes
Patient processes consist of treatments that patients go through, to be cured
from their disease. A patient process describes the ﬂow a patient goes through,
from the beginning when entering the hospital system till the end where the
patient is cured or suﬀers fatality. Patient processes describe the process for
one speciﬁc disease only and the process can have a deadline, such that the
patient must receive treatment before a certain deadline. The process can have
dependency constraints, such that certain treatments must happen before other
treatments. Treatments can sometimes occur at the same time. An example
can be that a blood sample testing is happening with a laborant, while the
the patient is having a consultation with a doctor. Patient processes can be
interrupted by other patient processes, such that prioritization can happen.
This occurs today, such that patients with deadlines can be prioritized before
others, but this can have a cost on the patient satisfaction.
2.1.4.1
Treatment
A patient process can be seen as a list of treatments, where each treatment has
a treatment type. The treatment type can either be a consultation, examina-
tion, surgery or admission. Though for the admission treatment type, other
treatments can occur at the same time, since an admission does not demand
the patient to lie still, unless explicitly speciﬁed. Treatment types often have a
speciﬁc department or sub-department attached to it, as departments are spe-
cialized in the given treatment type. An example can be given that a patient
receives surgery at one department, but the same department does not handle
admissions, which is why the admission treatment type has a diﬀerent depart-
ment for execution. A treatment is atomic, meaning that once a treatment has
begun, it cannot be interrupted or cancelled.
2.2
Multiprocessor system
The multiprocessor system is the general term for the entire system that operates
around and with a multiprocessor, where the multiprocessor is an entity in
the system that consist of several processors to execute jobs. The job for the
multiprocessor system is to coordinate shared resources among the processors
of a multiprocessor.
==PAGE:20==
10
Fundamentals of the Hospital- and Multiprocessor System
2.2.1
Processor
Processors are located inside the multiprocessor entity, which exists inside the
multiprocessor system.
The processors are the units executing tasks for the
multiprocessor system.
The processors are heterogeneous, as determined in
Section 1.4, meaning that they all execute diﬀerent types of tasks and they
cannot do each others job.
2.2.2
Resources
In the multiprocessor system, many types of resources exist, which all are well-
known today in computer systems. The multiprocessor system uses buses to
transport data between entities, hard disks to store data for long time and
memory to store data shortly. Resources are limited in the multiprocessor sys-
tem, and each resource usage has its costs in terms of kWH, but also a cost in
time for transport between entities. Most resources in a multiprocessor system
are shared among all the other processors. For example, the mentioned hard
disk which can be used by all processors. Though other resources, such as a
local processor cache, can only be used by the processor the cache belongs to.
2.2.3
Process
A process is very generally described as a list of tasks. Some processes can have
a deadlines for the completion of the whole process, while some processes never
end. The never ending processes often consist of periodic tasks which run in
intervals. The list of tasks can have dependency constraints, but if they do not,
a process can have tasks running simultaneously on several diﬀerent processors.
Processes can be interrupted in between tasks, such that other processes can be
prioritized, to meet their deadlines.
2.2.4
Task
The task in a multiprocessor is an atomic entity that consist of some lines of
code, which is to be processed by the multiprocessor, hence one or more of its
processors. Since the task is atomic, it cannot be split apart, meaning that once
it has started executing, it cannot be stopped until done. Tasks are often not
very big, but put together in a process, the list of tasks can be very large.
==PAGE:21==
2.3 Similarities between the systems
11
Periodic and sporadic tasks
Often in a multiprocessor system, tasks are deﬁned as being periodic.
The
periodic task has a regular time interval, in which the task is to be executed.
This could for example be a sensor reading or system monitoring.
Periodic
tasks have the advantage when scheduling, that the tasks are well deﬁned and
the scheduler knows when tasks are going to arrive. The interval of a periodic
task also deﬁnes the deadlines for the task. Since a periodic task must run in
intervals, a running periodic task must ﬁnish, before the next interval of the
same task is starting. Sporadic tasks are diﬀerent than periodic, in the way
that these have no speciﬁc interval, and the tasks can arrive at any given time.
The sporadic tasks can have, but not necessarily, deadlines and these are given
speciﬁc for the task.
2.3
Similarities between the systems
This section will compare the elements and properties, of the the hospital- and
multiprocessor systems described in Section 2.1 and 2.2. The comparison will
help the discussion on the beneﬁts of the analogy, while also help clarifying
which scheduling methods work well for the hospital system.
2.3.1
Elements
As a hospital system consist of several physical hospitals, the hospital system
can match the multiprocessor system, with the physical hospital being a mul-
tiprocessor unit. This includes, that department and sub-departments can be
seen as processors from the multiprocessor unit. As the processors are hetero-
geneous, they match the departments and sub-departments even better, since
these have focus on a speciﬁc part of the medical science. So departments are
not able to cure patients who belong to another department, in the same matter
as the heterogeneous processors cannot execute each others tasks.
Resources in both systems are very similar, as they both can be private and
shared resources. In terms of personal and equipment resources, personal re-
sources can be seen as the core parts of a processor that execute tasks. Equip-
ment resources can for example be seen as a hard disk, which acts as a ward for
patients. Practically, the hard disk would have to be divided into parts, as each
department often has a sub-department being the ward. Likewise, the main
memory in a multiprocessor system can be seen as a waiting room for patients
to be treated. If combining multiprocessors, a network card can be seen as the
==PAGE:22==
12
Fundamentals of the Hospital- and Multiprocessor System
connection between two physical hospitals, who both represent a multiprocessor
unit.
A process is similar to a patient process, where the process consist of tasks and
a patient process consist of treatments, making tasks the same as treatments.
Treatments, are like tasks, atomic, such that they cannot be stopped once ex-
ecution has begun. A CT-scanning does not make sense to stop once it has
started, unless it is really critical. Processes can, like patient processes, have a
ﬁnal deadline for the complete process and deadlines on each task in the pro-
cess. Processes in each system can be interrupted if prioritization is needed on
deadlines.
In multiprocessor systems, tasks are often deﬁned as being periodic, while treat-
ments in a hospital systems are most often sporadic. Treatments can for one
patient give one result, leading to another treatment, while for another patient,
it could give another result, leading to a diﬀerent treatment. Treatments can
though be periodic, for example when patients go to regular checks after they
have been cured from a disease.
2.3.2
Properties
While only loosely described in Section 2.1 and Section 2.2, both systems have
properties that also need to be compared to complete the analogy.
Deadlines exist in both systems and these can either be soft or hard deadlines.
Deadlines in the multiprocessor system can be hard such that the system is
interpreted as failed, if a deadline is not met.
Soft deadlines will not mark
the system as failed but might have consequences for the system. These conse-
quences could for example be a wrong result, while if many soft deadlines are not
met, the whole system could end up being interpreted as failed. Hard deadlines
in the hospital system does not necessarily mean that the system is interpreted
as failed. It could have economics consequences for the hospital system as they
do not hold their deadlines. It could have the more severe consequence, that pa-
tients might die, because they needed treatment before the deadline to survive.
This is seen in cancer packages, where early treatment is guarantied to ensure
high survival rates. Soft deadlines in the hospital system, do not have direct
consequences for the hospital system. But it could eﬀect the reputation of the
system, which could happen when patients get low satisfaction from treatments.
Another soft deadline could be the response time from the hospital. Response
time here relates to how fast a hospital allows for patients to be treated. If the
response times are high, the quality of the hospital decreases.
==PAGE:23==
2.4 Scheduling methods
13
There are also the properties of a multiprocessor system, called worst and best
case execution times. The worst case execution time is the maximum amount of
time a task can use in the system, while a best execution time is the minimum
amount a task has to use in the system to be able to ﬁnish. With deadlines,
the worst execution time, is the time diﬀerence between the beginning of a task
to the deadline. The best execution time is predeﬁned for the task. Comparing
this with the hospital system, worst case execution time is the same as the
multiprocessor system, while a best case execution time is as fast as possible.
When prioritizing processes, a multiprocessor system uses deadlines to organize
and schedule. When executing a set of processes, the multiprocessor might re-
ceive a new task, by another process, that has an earlier deadline, before one of
the current processes in execution. This could be, that the whole process has
an earlier deadline, or one of the tasks in the incoming process, has an earlier
deadline than tasks in execution. If preempting is allowed, the multiprocessor
breaks the processing of one of the current processes, when possible, sets it as
waiting, and starts executing the newly arrived process. While no documenta-
tion could be found on how the hospitals schedules and prioritize their patients,
it states at their info sites from outpatient clinics, that waiting time could arise
if critical patients arrive [18].
So similar to the multiprocessor system, very
critical patients get access if needed. When scheduling patients in the future,
the hospital system might leave blank appointments in the schedule. Such that
when patients with deadlines arrive, these can be ﬁtted into the blank appoint-
ments. No documentation could be found on this either, but it seems ﬁt for the
hospital system, as it is also practiced in [14].
2.4
Scheduling methods
The comparison made in Section 2.3, shows similarities between the two systems.
Hence the following will describe some scheduling methods, which are used in
a multiprocessor system and might seem ﬁt for the hospital system.
Other
scheduling methods exists for the multiprocessor system, while the focus in this
thesis has been the scheduling methods mentioned in this section.
2.4.1
First in ﬁrst out (FIFO)
FIFO is a the most simple scheduling method that can be used, and it is also the
most fair scheduling. FIFO neglects all prioritization, and allows for the ﬁrst
process to request a resource, to be the ﬁrst process allowed access to the re-
==PAGE:24==
14
Fundamentals of the Hospital- and Multiprocessor System
source. It ensures that at some point in time, any process will be granted access
to a resources, whether or not the process has a deadline. Thought it does not
ensure that these deadlines are held. FIFO serves as a great standard scheduling
method, to compare with possible optimization’s from other scheduling meth-
ods. An analysis of the diﬀerent scheduling methods, can show that FIFO might
actually be the best scheduling method to be used. This depends heavily on
what parameters the analysis is based on. FIFO is most commonly known as a
non-preemptive scheduling method, as if it is preemptive, the scheduling method
cannot be described completely as FIFO.
2.4.2
Shortest job ﬁrst (SJF)
SJF is a scheduling method which lets the shortest job, hence the job with
the shortest execution time, to be the ﬁrst job, to get access to a resource.
Prioritization is based on the length of the job, as scheduling is done when
the resource is available for the next job. This might cause the state where
jobs of long length to never get access to a resource, if jobs with shorter length
keep arriving and being ready when a resource is available. Deadlines risk not
being met, as deadlines can be placed on long jobs which never get access to a
resource. Diﬀerent perspectives can be used with the SJF scheduling method,
as it does ﬁt well for jobs where the length is not very long, while combined
with long jobs, it might not ﬁt very well. SJF can be both non-preemptive and
preemptive. Non-preemptive could cause starvation for short jobs, if a long job
has been allowed execution, such that short jobs cannot get access. On the
contrary, preemptive SJF could result in unfairness for long jobs, as it might
never ﬁnish execution because it is always interrupted.
2.4.3
Fixed priority scheduling (FPS)
FPS scheduling deﬁnes a static priority on all predeﬁned tasks. This priority is
then used to choose which task to execute next when the resource is available.
Similar to SJF, prioritization can risk leaving task with low priority behind,
such that these will never get access to the resource. Deadlines have a higher
chance of being met with the FPS scheduling method, as tasks with deadlines
often have a high priority in the system. FPS seems ﬁt for systems where task
with high priority are more common than tasks with low priority. If the arrival
of task with high priority is low, the scheduling method might also seem ﬁt
for low priority tasks. FPS can also be non-preemptive and preemptive. Like
with SJF, high priority tasks with deadlines risk not meeting their deadlines, if
non-preemptive is active and a low priority task is executing. On the contrary,
==PAGE:25==
2.4 Scheduling methods
15
if FPS is preemptive, low priority tasks might never complete execution, as high
priority tasks are interrupting the execution.
2.4.4
Earliest deadline ﬁrst (EDF)
While SJF and FPS uses the length or priority of a task to prioritize a resource
usage, EDF uses the deadline given for a task to prioritize. Hence, the next task
to be executed for a resource, is the task with the shortest deadline. Deadlines
risk not being met, if there are not enough resource usage time available to
execute a task, which is why EDF is often being implemented as preemptive.
EDF seems ﬁt for systems where deadlines are a part of it, but even with deadline
prioritization, deadlines risk not being met if to many tasks with deadlines
arrive. If the system is able to handle the incoming tasks with deadlines, the
system might not be able to handle tasks without, leading to unfairness in the
scheduling.
==PAGE:26==
16
Fundamentals of the Hospital- and Multiprocessor System
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 3
Design
Design choices made for the later implementation and analysis will be described
in this chapter.
This includes the design of the hospital model, the patient
arrivals distribution and the simulation for the diﬀerent scheduling methods.
Each part of the model will have a discussion to why the design choices were
made but also what limitations the design choices give. Distribution of patient
arrivals is needed to be able to simulate, which is why the chosen distribution
will be described. The principles of scheduling patients in the hospital system,
with a speciﬁc scheduling will be outlined. A discussion for each scheduling
method will also explain what consequences the method has for patients.
The following design will include code snippets from F#, where type decla-
rations for the model will be shown. Functions signatures for scheduling and
simulation will also be included.
3.1
Model
This section describes the model that will be used throughout the rest of the
thesis.
==PAGE:28==
18
Design
3.1.1
General
For the general purpose of the model, Listing 3.1 shows the general types deﬁned.
Instead of using the built-in System.DateTime in .NET, a more simple date and
time type are deﬁned, as each part will be used most often separately through the
model. This also applies for the simulation, where date and time are often used
without each other. With the custom date and time, unwrapping the type and
getting values is made more simple. The other general types deﬁned include an
address for hospitals and a speciﬁc department address. The department address
might include a building, ﬂoor or a speciﬁc room. These are interesting types to
include, as it might be needed to calculate distance between departments, when
transportation with a porter is relevant.

3.1.2
Personal
As being one of the two types of resources in the hospital system, the personal
is described in Listing 3.2. There exist several diﬀerent profession types in the
hospital system, and only the most well known and common ones are listed and
used in the model. Doctors can have diﬀerent titles and specialties (for a speciﬁc
medical science), together with surgeons and nurses. In reality, doctors might
even be surgeons also, and nurses might be nurses for a surgery but also for
an admission department. For the simplicity, but not irreversibly, the doctors,
surgeons and nurses are kept apart in the model.
To extend the design of the personal, a personal is having only one profession,
==PAGE:29==
3.1 Model
19
while for future development this could be extended to become a list of profes-
sions. All personal are kept in an dictionary, where the key value is a unique ID
for each personal. This makes for an easy and fast look-up of personal.

3.1.3
Equipment
As being the second of the two types of resources in the hospital system, equip-
ment is described in Listing 3.3. Personal as described in Section 3.1.2 has been
given a profession, to determine what the personals function is.
Equipment
does not have speciﬁc function other than what they are. With this, an equip-
ment is being given an equipment type, to determine what its function is. The
equipment type can be anything from a bed to a scalpel and an equipment has
an optional duration, as not all equipment has a speciﬁc duration deﬁned with
them. An example of an equipment with a speciﬁc duration is a CT-scanner,
that normally takes 20 minutes without preparation[1]. An example without a
duration is a bed.
Each equipment also has an optional operator with them. The operator is a
function to determine if a given profession is allowed to use the equipment,
hence the function signature becomes Profession -> bool. All equipment is
kept in a dictionary, where the key value is an unique ID for each equipment.
This makes for an easy and fast look-up of equipment.

Operator option

3.1.4
Hospital
The Hospital in the model is based upon one physical hospital. The physical
hospital as described in Section 2.1 is often divided into several departments
==PAGE:30==
20
Design
which then have further sub-departments. This structure of the physical hos-
pital is being kept in the model, such that there is a close relation to a real
physical hospital. In Petersen (2017), a sub department (department) can have
sections under it, but for simplicity in this model, the organization depth is kept
at 2 [14].
At each organizational level, there exists both personal and equipment. The
hospital management does not belong to a speciﬁc department so they exist at
the hospital level. A doctor specialized in cardiology, is only used by the de-
partment which specializes in cardiology. Speciﬁc equipment such as a scalpel
is only related to a surgery sub-department for surgeries. A bed can be used by
any department, but the beds are probably kept at hospital level, and instead
reserved for departments. The personal and equipment is added as a Set<Id>.
Each sub-department has its own department address, where each department
also has a department address but also a dictionary of all the sub-departments
under the department. The hospital then keeps a dictionary of all the depart-
ments. All hospital are kept in a dictionary where an ID uniquely identiﬁes the
hospital. The hospital dictionary can then be seen as an area from Section 2.1.

3.1.5
Patient process
For a patient process to be described, several diﬀerent treatment types need to
be deﬁned. This is shown in Listing 3.5, where four very common treatment
types are deﬁned:
Consultation, Examination, Surgery and Admission. Each
treatment type have properties which are:
• string: A description of the treatment in a common language.
==PAGE:31==
3.1 Model
21
• Id: A unique ID for either a hospital, department or sub-department.
• ExpectedDuration: An expected duration is included on all of the treat-
ment types, to indicate how long a reservation should be made for a pos-
sible appointment.
• NoLaterThan: A property that deﬁnes the deadline of the treatment type,
saying that the treatment must happen before this deadline in days. This
does not make sense for an admission, as admission often happens after a
surgery or when a long treatment is initiated.
• List<Equipment>: The list of equipment that is used in the treatment type.
• List<Profession>: The list of professions needed to complete the treat-
ment type. This is not used on an admission as admissions only rely on
equipment. If any profession is used in an admissions, it is done through
another treatment type, such as an Examination.
Additionally treatment types, Entrance and Exit, are used for simpliﬁcation of
the treatment process tree. All patient processes are thought of as being a part
of a tree, where a path from the root to a leaf is a patient process described
in treatment types - see Figure 3.1. The treatment type Entrance is added for
completing the whole patient process tree for a hospital system. It should be seen
as the part, where a patient receives a reference from a general practitioner to
get an appointment inside the hospital system. If the Entrance is not there, each
patient process would be described as a tree with only one path. Completing the
whole tree with Entrance, only simpliﬁes it without altering anything. Another
simpliﬁcation could be, to gather all the Exit vertices.
The tree is not weighted and choosing a path to go, is done from the treatment
outcome. This can be seen in Listing 3.5, from Tree<’a, ’o>, where Set<’o> is
the possible outcomes and the function ’o −> int determines which sub tree
from the Tree<’a, ’o> list should be chosen.
For further design, it is important to know how the process tree is constructed.
This can be seen in Listing 3.5 where Treatment is deﬁned as being the process
tree to be used. Here the TreatmentType is used as the treatment description,
while a string is used as the outcome value. The string is then to be written as
common language, the same way the description string from TreatmentType is
done.

2

3.1.6
Patient journal
The patient journals hold all the patient processes and treatments that a patient
goes through. A journal entry is created for each event, and this entry consist
of a JournalTreatmentType - which can be seen in Listing 3.6. Each entry has
several properties which are:
• string: Two strings exist where one is the description from the treatment
type, as described in Section 3.1.5, and another is a copy of the outcome,
that the given treatment type had.
• Id: The unique department ID from the treatment type.
• Set<Id>: A set for personal and equipment IDs that have performed the
treatment.
• Date: The original date, which is when the treatment type originated.
From this date, it was possible to get an appointment for this treatment
type, but as it is not always possible to get an appointment the same day,
this original date is kept for logging purposes.
• Time ∗Date: Two time and dates are added to describe the actual ap-
pointment for when the treatment was performed.
• AppointmentHistory: This property contains all earlier appointments with
their respective personal and equipment IDs, including the ﬁnal completed
appointment. The property is updated when a patient get an appointment
moved, for example when a deadline needs to be met for another patient.
This is done for logging purposes.
• Treatment: The original treatment as mentioned in Section 3.1.5. This is
added for logging purpose.
As mentioned in Section 2.1.4.1, an admission is not atomic, so other treat-
ment can be completed while an admission is active. For that reason, there
exist a JournalAdmissionStart which only has one time and date for the be-
ginning of the admission, and a JournalAdmissionEnd which only has on time
and date for the ending of the admission. This is also the reason for why an
JournalAdmissionEnd does not have an original date, outcome string and an
appointment history.
A patient journal is constructed as a list of JournalTreatmentType together with
a ’Globally Unique Identiﬁer’ (Guid). The GUID comes from the appointment
in the hospital calendar, which is described in Section 3.1.7. It is worth noticing
==PAGE:34==
24
Design
that an admission is only one appointment, meaning that in the patient journal,
a JournalAdmissionStart entry and a JournalAdmissionEnd entry has the same
GUID in its tuple. All patient journals are gathered in a dictionary, where a
patient ID is the key and the value is a tuple of a patient description and the
patient journal.

3.1.7
Hospital calendar
The hospital calendar keeps track of all appointments that exist in the past and
in the future. As seen in Listing 3.7, a Schedule is deﬁned as being a dictionary
where a date is the key. The date is kept as the key, making it easy to look
up any appointments that exist on a speciﬁc date, hence for looking up in the
future when an appointment can be booked. If a schedule does not contain a
date key, the resource cannot be used on that date. This can help in planning
a repairing of a CT-scanner or help the simulation in stating that equipment is
broken, such that it cannot be used on speciﬁc days.
For each date, another dictionary is used, where a time interval and GUID tuple
is used as the key. The time interval speciﬁes the speciﬁc appointment while
the GUID uniquely deﬁnes the appointment. An interval is uniquely deﬁned
to match it with other resources that are used in the appointment. This can
be seen in Listing 3.7, where a PersonalCalendar and a EquipmentCalendar is
deﬁned for both type of resources. Each resource then has it own schedule, but
some resources are used together to form a complete appointment. For example,
==PAGE:35==
3.1 Model
25
an admission might require a bed, bed table and a heart monitor. While two dif-
ferent admission appointments might have the same time interval, on the same
dates, the GUIDs are used to make sure that the correct bed, bed table and
heart monitor are combined, when looking up an appointment in the calendar.
At the same time, the GUID also indicates which time intervals belong to each
other to complete an appointment over several days. The GUID is kept in the
key for easier searching on speciﬁc appointments.
For each time interval and GUID key, a 3-tuple containing the patient ID, the
original date, as described in Section 3.1.6 for the appointment, and the treat-
ment, as described in Section 3.1.5, is stored as the value. The original dates
purpose is logging when an appointment had originated, such that if the appoint-
ment is to be moved at some point in the future, it does not pass a deadline
from the original date. The treatment is kept to describe which type of treat-
ment this appointment is, while also having references to all the next possible
treatments in the current patient process.
The case where an appointment exist over several days, an admission for exam-
ple, the model describes this as having a time interval ((10,0),(24,0)) on the
ﬁrst day, ((0,0),(24,0)) on the middle day and ((0,0),(10,0)) on the last day
for an appointment that exist over 2 days. Each time interval are then stored at
their respective dates, and the GUID is the same for all stored time intervals.
As mentioned in Section 3.1.4, all types of resources can exist on all organiza-
tional levels. To match this, a resource calendar for both personal and equip-
ment is kept on all levels, as shown in Listing 3.7, where a department has a
dictionary of all the sub-departments calendars and a hospital has a dictionary
of all department calendars. A dictionary gathers all hospital calendars for each
physical hospital.
1

Treatment>>
2

3

6

Listing 3.7: Type deﬁnitions for the hospital calendar, which also includes
the department and sub-department calendars.
==PAGE:36==
26
Design
3.1.8
Hospital shift calendar
To match a realistic hospital system, a hospital shift calendar has also been
added to the model. The shift calendar is used to indicate when personal has
speciﬁc shifts, such that appointments are placed correctly according to the
shifts, when appointments have personal included. Equipment resources do not
have shifts, as equipment do not have any requirements to shifts and working
hours.
An equipment is always available, but when an equipment is to be
used together with personal, as is the example with a CT-scanner. where a
radiographer is needed to operate the machine, the equipment can only be used
at a point where a radiographer is available.
For readability reasons, a type called ShiftSpan is added in Listing 3.8, even
though it is the same as a TimeInterval from Section 3.1.1. The same Listing
shows a ProfessionShiftCalendar, that in the same ways as a schedule from
Section 3.1.7, is a dictionary where a date is the key. The date maps to a value,
where a dictionary using a personal profession as key, maps further into a new
dictionary that uses shift spans as the key. For each shift span key, a set of
personal IDs are stored as the value, indicating which personal has the speciﬁc
shift.
In the same way as calendars from Section 3.1.7, there exist a shift calendar
for each sub-department, department and hospital.
A dictionary gathers all
hospital shift calendars for each physical hospitals.

(to−hour, to−minutes))
2

Map<ShiftSpan, Set<Id>>>>

3.1.9
Discussion and limitations
The design choices made for the model gives the result as shown from Section
3.1.1 to Section 3.1.8. These design choices could have been diﬀerent, and can
be taken into consideration if the model should ﬁt a speciﬁc hospital system.
==PAGE:37==
3.2 Distribution
27
Some of the design choices have already been discussed in the speciﬁc sections,
while the following discusses some general parts of the model:
• Personal are very ﬁxed to a speciﬁc department. In reality, a nurse for
example, could be working in diﬀerent departments on diﬀerent days, while
the model only indicates that the nurse is allocated to one department.
It is possible that the nurse can be added to several departments, but a
shift calendar must ensure that the nurse is not allocated in two diﬀerent
appointments at the same time.
• The organization structure and depth is very constrained, while in reality
it might be even bigger. Though this does not prevent analysis being made
on this speciﬁc hospital system modelled.
• Patient processes are described in concrete boxes while in reality, this
might not be possible. This is taken into consideration, allowing for inﬁnite
possibilities of outcomes and future treatment types. Though constructing
a complete tree for all possible patient processes seems impractical.
• The case where a patient goes through a process which is not the correct
one, and must start a new process to try for another correct one, introduces
several processes for one patient. This has been considered thoroughly to
match reality, and can be seen as a patient going through one path in
the process tree, which did not result in the correct outcome, hence the
patient starts at another path in the tree.
• The tree describing possible patient process could be extended with a cost
on each edge, that could be used for decision making. The cost would
be in terms of money for the hospital system, and could be used for the
hospital to make further patient treatment decisions based on cost as well.
As money cost has not been the focus on this thesis, this has not been
added.
• In theory, personal could be given shifts in the model that exist over several
days. This is most often not the case in reality, as work environment rules
would be violated. So it is up to the person ﬁlling the shift calendar to
take care of this.
3.2
Distribution
To be able to simulate, patient arrivals must be generated for the hospital sys-
tem. The type of data needed to simulate, is an amount of patient arrivals, for
==PAGE:38==
28
Design
each type of patient process, on each future date to be simulated. The patient
arrival data would be most realistic, if it could be generated from statistics on
past data. This has not been available for this thesis, so the generation must
be done theoretically. A uniform distribution could be used to generate patient
arrivals. This would give all possible outcomes of number, of patient arrivals,
an equal chance to be generated. While the uniform distribution is most fair,
a Poisson distribution allows for a given average rate of arrivals per time slot
to inﬂuence the number generated. This makes the Poisson distribution most
ﬁt for the simulation of patient arrivals, as it can used with future collection of
historical data to inﬂuence the patient arrivals. Hence if data was to be given
of earlier patient arrivals, the average rate could be used from this to generate
future arrivals.

2

3

4

5

6

7

Listing 3.9: The function signature for generating patient arrivals, for future
dates with a Poisson distribution.
Listing 3.9 shows the function signature for generating patient arrivals with a
Poisson distribution. The function takes in a date interval for dates to generate
values for, while also getting a random function to be used. A list of ﬂoats
and patient processes, which must have the same length, represent the speciﬁc
lambda values to be used with the Poisson distribution. This allows for unique
distributions for each type of patient process. The function returns a dictionary,
where a date as the key determines for which date the patients arrivals should
be used, and a list of future patients is given as the value for each date. For
each patient in this list, a patient ID, description and patient process is given.
3.3
Scheduling
Section 2.3 shows that similarities exist between the two systems, making it
interesting to see what the possibilities are for scheduling a set of patients. The
following sections include a section for diﬀerent aspects of scheduling patients,
and sections for each scheduling method considered to be valid for patients.
==PAGE:39==
3.3 Scheduling
29
3.3.1
Aspects
When scheduling patients, certain aspects of the scheduling method have to be
considered before choosing a valid one. These aspects are the following, where
a ﬁnal discussion will elaborated on how each aspect aﬀects the other aspects.
Patient satisfaction
Patient satisfaction within with the hospital system is one of the ways to measure
the performance of a hospital system. If a hospital system performs optimal, the
patient satisfaction is high in terms of low waiting time for appointments, good
coordination between the personal and also good coordination and communica-
tion between the personal and the patient. If these terms are all acceptable for
a patient, the resulting patient process is quick and smooth, with hopefully a
cure at the end of the process. If one or more terms are not acceptable, the pa-
tient risk of having a low satisfaction, because of very long waiting times or bad
communication with the personal about the process. The long waiting can exist
both when arriving at the hospital, but also when receiving an appointment in
the future. A patient might not want to wait 180 days before being treated, so
a maximum number of waiting days should ensure that the patient has some
good experience.
Moving around with appointments for patients can also be the cause for low
satisfaction.
A patient might not accept getting an appointment moved the
same day that the appointment is scheduled, as the patient might have already
turned up at the hospital for the treatment. But if an appointment, that is
scheduled several days in the future, is moved, the patient might accept this as
it does not have the largest inﬂuence on the patient. Going even further, one
could imaging that a patient got an appointment moved ﬁve times, such that
the patient might have to wait 180 days before being treated.
Costs
As another performance measure, costs determines how good the hospital system
is to use its resources. Correctly spent money on resources that are needed,
increases the patient put-through as the resources needed comes from increasing
patient arrivals. The money a hospital system has is ﬁnite, so the money needs
to be sorted and used correctly. But as the money is ﬁnite, so is the resources,
so to perform well, the scheduling method needs to make sure that resources
are used optimal.
Cure ability
Cure ability measures how good the hospital system is to cure patients. This
is closely related to patient satisfaction, but can by itself also be a performance
measurement for the hospital system.
==PAGE:40==
30
Design
Patient put-through
Closely related to patient satisfaction and cure ability, another measurement on
the hospital system is how many patients are put through the system. This
performance measurement can have several variations, where one very good put
through could be that many patients are cured. This can be caused by patients
that are not very sick or have simple treatments, so the put-through is high.
But if patients are very sick and it takes a long time to be cured, the patient
put-through is not very high, if the put-through is based on number of patients.
If the put-through is based on very sick patients, where the general survival rate
is low, but the survival rate is high for the hospital system, the put-through is
also high.
Discussion
When considering all the aspects mentioned above, each aspect can have an
inﬂuence on the other aspects.
Lowering the expectations for good patient
satisfaction, such as increasing the number of days a patient is allowed to wait
for an appointment, can reduce the costs for the hospital system. In this case,
the hospital system might not need to purchase new resources to ﬁt waiting time
deadlines. Turning it around, if costs can be increased, the patient satisfaction
has chances of getting better. Higher costs might also be justiﬁable if the patient
satisfaction is very good at the same time, with the opposite not being true.
Simultaneously, cure ability and patient put-through can be increased with more
resources, while lowering costs decreases both. But depending on the patient
put-through variation, low costs might not aﬀect the patient put-through, but
decrease the patient satisfaction for critical diseases. The put-through of these
would not be very high, as expensive equipment cannot be bough, hence the
cure ability for the hospital system to cure critical diseases is low.
This thesis does not focus on what aspects are important, neither are any chosen
to be the most important. The purpose is though, to choose and show scheduling
methods that can provide diﬀerent possibilities between the aspects.
These
diﬀerent aspects will be used in the next sections, to show how the scheduling
methods aﬀect each one of them. It is important to note that each scheduling
method has diﬀerent outcomes, depending on the number of arriving patients.
Some scheduling methods prioritize patients over others, which can be good,
if there are few prioritized patients arriving. It can also be bad for patients
without prioritization, if many patients with prioritization arrive. This is also
considered in the next sections about scheduling methods.
==PAGE:41==
3.3 Scheduling
31
3.3.2
FIFO
The ’First-In-First-Out’ scheduling method as described in Section 2.4.1 is the
most fair scheduling method for all patients, in terms of getting an appointment.
According to the aspects given in Section 3.3.1, the scheduling method allows
for a good patient satisfaction in general. Patients are not being forgotten and
pushed back in line, neither are they waiting to long to get an appointment,
depending on the amount of patient arrivals and resources given.
Costs are
being made optimal, as the money received is divided equally, to ensure that
all departments can cope with the statistically amount of patients arriving at
each department. Cure ability and patient put-through, can depending on the
type of patient arrivals, be at any level. While FIFO ensures fairness, the cure
ability and patient put-through is at most on an average level for FIFO.
FIFO serves as a great starting point for scheduling patients, being it is the
most fair scheduling. With patient process deadlines, nor maximum days before
getting an appointment, not respected, FIFO can have consequences for the
hospital system as they are not coping with they deadlines. Hence FIFO should
not be a preferred scheduling method, but it can used to compare with other
scheduling methods, to see how good they perform.
Listing 3.10 shows the function signature for scheduling with FIFO. The function
takes the diﬀerent model types as HospitalCalendar and PatientJournals with
it, making it possible to begin a simulation on an already existing simulation
result. Further more, the function takes a starting date and how many days
there should be simulated. A process tree is also given together with a list of
ﬂoats for each process, such that the Poisson distribution is unique for each
process. A seed for the random function used in the simulation is also given,
such that simulations can be redone with the same result. A ﬁnal parameter
given, is a reasonable look a head limit, which limits the days for looking ahead
for an appointment.
If no appointment can be found within this limit, the
simulation should fail as it is not possible to ﬁnd a solution with the given
parameters. This extra parameter should serve as fairness and can be given as
integer maximum if no fairness should be used. The function returns a string,
determining if the simulation was successful or if it failed at some point. The
resulting patient journals and hospital calendar is returned, as these are altered
through the simulation, together with a queue stack result, so the queue stack
can be analyzed to see if queues are adding up.
1
type StackResult = Map<Id, Map<Date, int>>
2

StackResult ∗StackResult ∗Map<Id, StackResult ∗StackResult>>

3.3.3
SJF
The SJF scheduling method from Section 2.4.2, is a method that prioritizes
jobs with lowest execution time ﬁrst. This scheduling method can result in two
diﬀerent patient satisfaction scenarios. One where several more patients with
short treatment duration’s arrive than patients with long treatment duration’s.
Here the patients with long treatment duration’s risk being forgotten and pushed
in line. These patients would then have low satisfaction while the other type of
patients would have high. The second scenario would be if few patients arrive
with short duration treatments. Here the patient satisfaction for long duration
treatments would be greater as their odds of getting an appointment is higher.
Costs could be lowered with this scheduling method as shorter treatments might
cost less for the hospital system. The cure ability and patient put-through would
be high, if many patients with short treatment duration’s arrive the most.
Even with the beneﬁts mentioned above for the SJF scheduling method, this
scheduling method does not seem ﬁt for the hospital system. Patients risk being
left behind because their treatment duration is too long, which does not seem
ﬁt for a hospital system, who’s purpose is to treat all types of patients. The
SJF scheduling method could be extended with a ’maximum days of waiting’
parameter, but the scheduling method would then be better described as ’earliest
deadline ﬁrst’, which is a scheduling method described later in Section 3.3.5.
Hence the shortest job ﬁrst scheduling method is not used in this thesis.
==PAGE:43==
3.3 Scheduling
33
3.3.4
FPS
FPS, as explained in Section 2.4.3, sets a priority on each process that does not
change at any given time. The general patient satisfaction is good with this
scheduling method, but it is very dependent on how the ﬁxed priority is set
for speciﬁc type of patients and how the arrivals are for these patients. FPS is
already seen in hospital systems, as critical patients (such as gun wounds) are
moved in front of others, even if a patients are supposed to be treated. Region
Sjælland even has a disclaimer saying that there might become waiting time, if
critical patients arrive [20].
For costs, the FPS method can end up costing more for the hospital. Priority
patients often require more health care than the regular patient without priority,
increasing the cost. With cure ability, it can be high if it is based on patients
with priority, but if it is the general patient type, then it would be lower than
average. The same applies for patient put-through, as patients without priority
risk being overseen.
Even though the above drawbacks are based on the priority positioning and
patient arrivals for priority patients, the scheduling model does not seem ﬁt for
the hospital system. FPS can be seen as the opposite of the SJF method, as
patients with short treatment duration are often prioritized lower than patients
that have long, hence because of the type of disease. With the same reason as
for the SJF method, that the purpose of the hospital system is to treat all type
of patients, FPS is not chosen as a scheduling method in this thesis. FPS could
be extended with a ’maximum days of waiting’ parameter the same way as SJF.
3.3.5
EDF
The EDF scheduling method from Section 2.4.4, prioritizes patients who have
the shortest deadline.
One very interesting and important patient group in
Region Sjælland is cancer patients, who since 1978 have increased in arrivals
[14]. Cancer packages in Denmark ensure that patients with cancer must be
treated before a certain deadline, making the patient group a perfect match
for the EDF scheduling method. To allow for patients to be prioritized before
others, preempting must be allowed such that patients with deadlines can skip
the queue and come in front. This also means that patients without deadlines
risk being moved to inﬁnite, such that the patient never gets an appointment.
To make sure that all patients get an appointment in a certain amount of time,
a maximum waiting time for patients can be applied, as mentioned in SJF and
FPS. This does give all patients deadlines and ensures a high patient satisfaction
==PAGE:44==
34
Design
for all patients. A low patient satisfaction could arise when a patient has an
appointment on the current day, and the appointment gets moved a week in
the future. The patient might have already arrived at the hospital, ready for
a treatment. In this case, the patient would have to go home and come back
a week later, risking that the patient will be moved again. To ensure that this
does not happen, EDF can receive the parameter that a minimum number of
days must have passed before moving patients in the future.
With a general deadline on all patients, the costs can be lowered if the result
is that deadlines are held. In the Danish health care sector, an extended free
hospital selection allows for patients to book appointments at private hospitals,
if the patient has to wait more than 30 days for a consultation, examination
or treatment[15]. This type of treatment guaranty costs extra for the Danish
health care sector[16], so lowering the uses of the guaranty will save money.
The cure ability is kept a little above average for EDF, as critical patients are
prioritized, giving them a good chance of surviving a disease. All non critical
patients are still prioritized also increasing the chance of being cured for these.
Patient put-through is kept at an average for as all type of patients are treated.
Extending the EDF scheduling method with the new parameters mentioned
above, serves as a great scheduling method for the hospital system.
EDF
scheduling can be executed with both hard and soft deadlines for patients. If
EDF is executed with hard deadlines, a solution might not exist for a given
set of parameters, while with soft deadlines a solution might exist but is not
optimal. An accepted maximum number of soft deadlines in a give time interval
can be set, to see if a better solution exists.
Listing 3.11 shows the function signature for scheduling with EDF, using hard
deadlines. The three dots ... represent the same function parameters FIFO
scheduling has in Listing 3.10, though with a maximum number of waiting days
given instead of the reasonable limit. This parameter is general deadline for all
patients. A minimum days that should pass before moving patients is also given,
together with a bool that determines if prioritized patients can move other pri-
oritized patients. Allowing prioritized patients moving other prioritized patients
might give a solution where one is not allowed, but can increase the number of
recursive movements that are done. The parameter movingPatientsDepthLimit
restricts the depth for moving appointments. It does so, by counting how many
appointments are being moved recursively, from the ﬁrst appointment and com-
paring it with the given parameter. The EDF scheduling with hard deadlines
returns the same result as FIFO.

2
... −>
3

==PAGE:45==

3.4
Simulation
The chosen scheduling methods mentioned in Section 3.3, all have some simi-
larities in terms of simulation. The next sections will describe the assumptions
made on simulating and show the general design for ﬁnding available appoint-
ments, and iterating appointments. These methods are used by all scheduling
methods.
3.4.1
Assumptions
To be able to perform simulations, certain assumptions are made for the design
choices in this chapter. These are listed as the following:
1. A treatment is always using an appointment with the same duration.
2. Manning the shift calendar is done randomly between all personal who
belong to a speciﬁc department.
3. Personal cannot be used over several days in appointments.
4. The next treatment after a surgery, in a patient process, must be an
admission.
5. Admitted patients can get the next treatment right after they have been
admitted.
6. Surgery cannot be the ﬁrst treatment in a patient process.
7. Admission appointments cannot be moved, unless these are moved to-
gether with the corresponding surgery appointment.
==PAGE:46==
36
Design
Assumption (1) says that any treatment with a given duration, will always
use that speciﬁc duration.
In reality, this might not be the case for a real
hospital system as for example a surgery can take less time if no complications
arise during surgery or might take the double time if many complications arise.
Assumption (2) only states that no working conditions have been taken into
consideration, such as vacation periods or lunch breaks, when manning the shift
calendar. Though a shift could be separated into several, like ((8,0),(11,30))
and ((12,0),(16,0)), such that a lunch break exists between 11:30 and 12:00.
Assumption (3) determines that treatments which include personal, can only
last a shift length. Shifts are separated per day, meaning that appointments
over days cannot be combined with shifts. This is often also the case in the real
world as personal rarely have shifts over several days. Assumption (4) only limits
the next treatment after a surgery to being an admission, as this is also often
the reality when a patient has a surgery. Assumption (5) allows for admitted
patients to get appointments right after the surgery has been completed, while
in reality this might not be case as the patient has to be awake and recover
a little before any examination. Assumption (6) has been made as a design
choice for simulation, as it requires both a surgery appointment and a admission
appointment, as explained in Section 3.4.2.3. This also reﬂect a real hospital
system, as patient often have a consultation before getting a surgery. Though it
does not require much to add the possibility to simulation. The last assumption
(7) is only a simpliﬁcation, which can be changed if needed.
3.4.2
Finding available intervals
Finding appointments is one of the most crucial parts of the simulation and it
is done for all treatments. The calendar model described in Section 3.1.7, shows
that a schedule is created for each resource, where appointments are organized
by dates. This means, that when ﬁnding possible appointments, each schedule
must be searched through, for each resource that can be used. The treatment
type in Section 3.1.5 holds two sets, where a needed set of personal is one and
equipment is another. The department to execute the treatment is also deﬁned,
so the searching of appointments can be narrowed down to a speciﬁc department.
3.4.2.1
Finding intervals in schedules
To ensure that running simulations is optimized as much as possible, ﬁnding
appointments must be optimized.
Since the duration is known for all types
of treatments, appointments could be found by traversing the schedule for each
==PAGE:47==
3.4 Simulation
37
resources and then adding a possible appointment to a list, each time an appoint-
ment is possible between other appointments. Figure 3.2(a) shows a schedule
where 3 appointments exist with space between each other. When traversing
the schedule, all possible appointments before, between and after each appoint-
ment would be added to the list, as shown in Figure 3.2(b). But as it can be
seen in Figure 3.2(b), unused time intervals between appointments can occur
if the interval length is not a multiple of an appointment duration, risking in
a utilization of resources that is not maxed out. To cope with unused inter-
vals, Figure 3.2(c) shows how intervals between all existing appointments, are
gathered instead of the speciﬁc possible appointments. With intervals instead,
appointments can be selected anywhere in the interval, as long as the duration
for an appointment does not exceed an interval boundary.

Listing 3.12 shows the function signature for extracting intervals in a schedule.
The function has the extension OverDays, as intervals are extracted over days.
The function takes the schedule from a speciﬁc resource as input, together with
a start- and ending date for extracting intervals. A start time is given, as the
extraction is based on an appointment that has just been ﬁnished, and an exact
starting time restriction is used for when appointments have to start at a speciﬁc
time, or no appointment can be used, see Section 3.4.2.3. Finally a duration is
added to check if intervals found are valid and a boolean determines if patients
can be moved. The function returns a list of available intervals, which have
the same format as an Appointment, and a dictionary of apps which should be
moved if this is allowed.
3.4.2.2
General appointments
The function extractIntervalsOverDays is able to extract intervals for a single
resource, while many patient treatment types can depend on many diﬀerent
type of resources being available at the same time. To cope with this, it should
be possible to get a list of available intervals for all resources needed, such that
an appointment can be selected from any set of resources that are possible the
speciﬁc department for a treatment.
Listing 3.13 shows the function signature for getting all available intervals in
a given time interval.
The ... represent the same as Section 3.3.5 together
with the parameters allowMovingOfPatients, prioritizedCanMovePrioritized,
minimumDaysBeforeMoving, movingPatientsDepthLimit and maximumWaitingDays.
Beyond this, the fetchAllIntervals is added as an optimization parameter. It
allows for only getting the ﬁrst possible interval, such that the searching for
appointment does not get a list of all available intervals, which indeed can be
very large. A date interval is given to the function to deﬁne the search interval
while also including an earliest time and exact earliest time parameter, which
can be translated into startTime and exactStartTime from Listing 3.12. The
treatment to search availability for, is given and the current count of recursive
iteration is also given, to compare with the limit in EDF scheduling. The func-
tion should return a list, where for each, a possible combination of personal and
equipment resources that match the given treatment is added. Further more,
==PAGE:49==
3.4 Simulation
39
there should be a list of available intervals. If the purpose is to ﬁnd a prioritized
appointment, the list of intervals is a list of appointments, as when a prioritized
appointment is found, it has moved around other appointments. That is why,
a hospital calendar and new patient journals are giving together for each ap-
pointment. If it is not a prioritized appointment, the hospital calendar and the
patient journals are the same as given to the function.

3.4.2.3
Surgery appointments
Surgery treatments diﬀer from other treatments as they require admission im-
mediately after the surgery is completed. This is also an assumption made in
Section 3.4.1, such that when an surgery appointment is searched for, an admis-
sion appointment is also searched for at the same time. Hence if no admission
is possible after a given surgery appointment, the given surgery appointment
cannot be used.
Listing 3.14 shows the function signatures for getting a surgery appointment
together with an admission appointment. The ... represent the same as Section
3.4.2.2 together with the fetchAllIntervals parameter. The two functions also
include the Treatment for surgery and admission. The intervals that have been
found possible for surgeries, are passed into iterateSurgeryIntervals, such that
these can be iterated to ﬁnd a possible admission appointment. These include
the updated hospital calendar and patient journals, if appointments have been
moved for prioritization.

3.4.3
Iterating appointments
Figure 3.3 shows the simulation ﬂow for simulating multiple dates and appoint-
ments. When simulating, all appointments on a given date is to be traversed, to
simulate that these are being executed on the speciﬁc date. Hence appointments
need to be gathered and sorted from the calendar, such that appointments with
the earliest ending time gets executed ﬁrst by the simulator. This gives the most
realistic situation, and Listing 3.15 shows the type deﬁnition for a dictionary,
where a Time as the key determines the ending time for an appointment, and
a value is a list of all appointments that end at that speciﬁc time, determined
by the key. Since a schedule exist for all resources, it is to be ensured that ap-
pointments are gathered correctly, such that each appointment is only executed
once. Listing 3.15 shows the two functions for collecting all appointments for
execution, where gatherAndSortAllAppointments gets all the appointments that
exist in the calender for a given date, and mergeGatheredAndSortedAppointments
combines two dictionaries of gathered appointments. Updating should be done
after each appointment execution, as an execution of an appointment can result
in a new appointment that exists on the same day - see Figure 3.3.
