==PAGE:53==
Chapter 4
Implementation
With a model and functions signatures from Chapter 3, this chapter will de-
scribe the implementations made to complete a simulation and an analysis.
The general structure of the implementation will be ﬁrst described, such that
when functions are referred to throughout the implementation, the placement
of these functions make sense. A section of general functions used by simulation
will be described thoroughly, while all functions used can be seen in Appendix
C, D, E, F, G, H and I. One section is included for each scheduling method, as
the scheduling methods have diﬀerent functions used, while some being general
and kept in the general function section.
Type annotations have been removed from F# code listings in this chapter to
give a less confusing view of the listings. Type annotations can be required for
accumulators to the F# compiler, so see the above mentioned appendices for
complete implementation, where type annotations also are.
4.1
Structure
When implementing F#, all functions could be placed together within the
same F# script ﬁle.
But to keep a better overview of where functions are
==PAGE:54==
44
Implementation
placed and relevance of these functions, the whole implementation is sepa-
rated into several F# source ﬁles, which then can be used by a script ﬁle
to execute functions.
All source ﬁles have been included in the namespace
Modelling_and_Analysis_of_Hospital_Systems with a module that has the same
name as the ﬁlename. The following lists the ﬁles that have been created to-
gether with an explanation of content in the source ﬁle:
• Model.fs: Keeps the model deﬁned in Section 3.1, with all the type deﬁ-
nitions and exception deﬁnitions used throughout the implementation.
• ModelHelper.fs: Contains functions to help use the model or extract
data from a HospitalCalendar for example, which contains calendars for
all departments. Also helps with comparing times, dates and intervals as
these have been custom deﬁned in Section 3.1.1.
• ModelFunctions.fs:
Contains functions for manipulating the model,
such as adding an appointment to the calendar or updating a patient
journal.
• DistributionHelper.fs: Holds functions for generating distributions.
• Analyzer.fs: Contains functions that help analyze given data from the
model. This includes functions for extracting the queue stack while simu-
lating but also sanity checking the resulting data.
• PrintHelper.fs: Has functions that could help showing relevant data,
while simulating but also after simulation, to the console.
• SimulatorHelper.fsi: Contains only the F# function signatures for the
simulator helper. Some of these signatures are described in Section 3.4.
• SimulatorHelper.fs: Contains the actual fuctions described in Simula-
torHelper.fsi but also other functions that are not described with a signa-
ture. Functions in this ﬁle are helper functions to simulate.
• Simulator.fsi: Hold the F# function signatures for the simulator. These
signatures are described in Section 3.3.
• Simulator.fs: Holds the actual functions described in Simulator.fsi, where
the speciﬁc simulator method can be selected.
• FileHelper.fs: Has functions that help printing speciﬁc data to a ﬁle,
such that a simulation result can be saved and used later.
==PAGE:55==
4.2 General functions
45
4.2
General functions
As mentioned in Section 3.4, all scheduling methods have functions that are
used in general. Some of these will be explained thoroughly together with the
generation of patient arrivals in a Poisson distribution.
4.2.1
Local type deﬁnitions
To help shorten function parameters in some functions, two type deﬁnitions
have been created locally in SimulatorHelper, such that functions can be writ-
ten with fewer parameters.
These are shown in Listing 4.1, where the type
IterationPack contains all the relevant model types needed when iterating ap-
pointments and the type IterationOpts contains all the general conﬁguration
options used througout the simulation. The options are the following:
• getAllPossibleIntervals (bool): A performance parameter used in EDF
scheduling. Section 4.4 explains this option in more detail.
• allowMovingOfPatients (bool): The conﬁguration that enable EDF
scheduling, such that possible future appointments are also where existing
appointments can be moved.
• prioritizedCanMovePrioritized (bool): The parameter explained in
Section 3.3.5, which allows for prioritized patients to move other prioritized
patients.
• minimumDaysBeforeMovingPatients (int): The parameter used in
scheduling EDF as explained in Section 3.3.5.
• patientMovingIterationLimit (int): Another parameter used in schedul-
ing EDF as explained in Section 3.3.5.
• maximumWaitingDays (int): The parameter used in scheduling EDF
but also acting as the parameter reasonableLookAheadLimit in scheduling
FIFO.

4.2.3
Comparing intervals
Comparing time intervals, also known as appointments as they have the same
structure, is a very crucial and demanding part of extracting appointments. To
be able to see if two or more resources can be used together in an appointment,
their available intervals must be compared for overlapping intervals to see if an
appointment is possible. As shown in Figure 4.2, where two sets of intervals
are given, the resulting list of overlapping intervals is [((t3,d3),(t8,d8));
((t9,d9),(t4,d4)); ((t5,d5),(t10,d10))].

it can be seen that overlapping intervals are ((t3,d3),(t8,d8)),
((t9,d9),(t4,d4)) and ((t5,d5),(t10,d10)).
The comparing of intervals becomes complicated when having both dates and
times, which demands for a very speciﬁc function on comparing intervals. This
is shown in Listing 4.3.
The function iterates over both interval lists given
at the same time, comparing their respective appointments and adding to the
accumulator if an overlapping appointment exists. As it can be seen in line 14, an
interval is used again in the recursive call as the interval has not been completely
compared. If either lists are empty, the function returns the accumulator as no
more overlapping intervals can exist. The ...’s in the listing represents more
code, but as the function is too large to have in this text, the complete functions
is shown in Appendix C. When initiating the nested function internalFunc,
the two lists given are ﬁltered for appointments that has no length, as these
appointments can disturb the comparison. The two lists are also sorted such
that comparison is done correctly.

4.2.4
Extracting intervals
When extracting intervals for a resource, the schedule for that resource must
be traversed to ﬁnd available intervals.
As mentioned in Section 3.4.2, ap-
pointments can be placed anywhere in a schedule, so to ensure that the correct
intervals are extracted, the schedule must be traversed completely every itera-
tion of ﬁnding an interval. By extracting intervals instead of appointment also
gives a little better performance, as the search should not be repeated for each
available appointment between existing appointments, but should only be done
once between the existing appointments.
Listing 4.4 shows the implementation of the function signature in Listing 3.12.
As it can be seen in the implementation, a nested function is used to add ap-
pointments that should be moved, in case of prioritizing, while another nested
recursive function, iterateSch, iterates the schedule. As intervals can span over
several days, an accumulator for the start date and start time is used together
==PAGE:59==

49
with an iteration count, such that the function knows when it is to use a new
accumulator for start date and time. Diﬀerent scenarios arise when looking at
schedules, as a schedule might not contain a date in the future, meaning that
no appointments are possible on that date. This is shown with the match of
trying to ﬁnd a date as the key in the schedule, and with the complete function
in Appendix G, it can be seen that well deﬁned intervals are added to the ac-
cumulator accuL and the accumulator for apps to be moved, accuM, is updated
when appointments can be moved.

4.2.5
Executing appointment
When iterating appointments, each appointment is executed one by one accord-
ing to their ending time, as explained in Section 3.4.3. Listing 4.5 shows the
implementation of the function signature that executes appointments in Listing
==PAGE:60==

3.15. If the patient is admitted before the execution, the current treatment type
is checked to see if it is another admission appointment being executed, hence
an admission often last over several days, and as appointments are saved per
date, an appointment exist on each date that an admission exist. So to not
execute the same admission appointment, the next date is checked for the same
admission appointment and if no exist and admission end is added to the patient
journal, while if one exists the day after, the current state is returned.
If the current treatment type being executed is a surgery appointment, the
journal is merely updated and returned with the current state. Assumption 4
in 3.4.1 says that an admission must be after a surgery and since the function
getSurgeryAndAdmissionAppointment in Section 3.4.2.3 gets both the surgery and
admission appointment, no new appointment is needed to be found.
Though if the current treatment is anything else than a surgery, and the patient
is not currently admitted, then the patient journal is updated and then if the
patient is admitted now, the next appointment is fetched with the earliest time
being the start of the admission (t1) - this is assumption 5 from 3.4.1. If the
patient is not admitted after execution of the current treatment type, hence
the current treatment type is not an admission start, the earliest time for next
appointment is after the current appointment (t2).

Depending on the outcome of the above, the function getNextAppointment is
called to get the next appointment, which also adds the next appointment to
the calendar and returns the state afterwards.
As it can be seen in Listing
4.5, the execution of an appointment generally only consist of updating the
patient journal. This is called update journal because when getting the next
appointment in getNextAppointment, the next appointment that is retrieved, is
also added to the patient journal. This is done for logging purposes, as the
appointment might be so far in the future that the simulator will never reach
the date, but in the patient journal, it can be seen that a future appointment
exists. The function getNextAppointment can be seen in Appendix G.
4.3
FIFO approach
Figure 4.3 shows the ﬂow of simulating multiple days with the FIFO scheduling.
The simulation begins with generating all the Poisson values needed for future
dates where it afterwards iterates all days to be simulated. For each day, the
simulator ﬁrst iterates the new incoming patients, ﬁnds an appointment, adds it
to the calendar and then continues by gathering all appointments for the speciﬁc
==PAGE:62==
52
Implementation
day. All the gathered appointments are then executed and when there exists
no more appointments for the current date, it increments the date and starts
again. If the ending date has been reached, the simulator returns the state and
stops simulating.
Gather all appointments 
for current date 
Iterate all appointments today
Begin simulation 
Last simulation  
date done 
Iterate all new requests
Increment 
current date
Generate poisson for 
all future dates 
Figure 4.3: The ﬂow diagram for simulating multiple days with FIFO.
Listing 4.6 shows the implementation of the function signature in Listing 3.10,
while the function also being the code representation of Figure 4.3. Here the
random number generator used throughout the simulator is being created, where
all the future incoming patient arrivals are generated. As it can be seen in line
6, opts are the conﬁguration options to be used in simulation. As explained in
Section 4.2.1, these options deﬁne the behaviour of the simulator, so when the
allowance of moving patient appointments is set to false, the simulator cannot
move any patient appointments, so it acts as a FIFO scheduling. With this set
to false, all the other options are ignored, with only reasonableLookaheadLimit
being used as described in Section 3.3.2.
The two nested functions iterateNewArrivals and simulate are doing the simu-
lation where simulate gathers appointments and executes them. When running
simulate, the whole code block is surrounded with try...with, to act on ex-
==PAGE:63==
4.3 FIFO approach
53
ceptions thrown in simulation. With FIFO, the reasonableLookaheadLimit can
make the simulation throw an LookaheadLimitExceededException exception, be-
cause the limit has exceeded. If this happens, the simulator stops and returns
the current state. If no exception is thrown, a solution exists for the given pa-
rameters, so the result is returned.
To see the rest of the function code, instead of ..., look in Appendix H.

might get appointment on the same day but they might also get appointments
in the future.

4.4
Hard EDF approach
The EDF approach to simulating the hospital system is very similar to FIFO
in terms of code. Hence Figure 4.3 describes EDF just as well as FIFO, while
the F# function being a little diﬀerent.
In Listing 4.8, it can be seen that
the conﬁguration options instead of false, includes a true for the allowance
of moving patients. Further more the function parameters given with the EDF
function are passed into the options where also maximumWaitingDays has replaced
reasonableLookaheadLimit from FIFO, as now all patients have a deadline as
described in Section 3.3.5.
The listing for EDF also shows an exception catching for NoPossiblePrioritized
AppointmentException, which is thrown when the simulator cannot ﬁnd an ap-
pointment for a prioritized patient before its deadline. As this is an EDF with
hard deadlines, the simulator must stop, as no solution exists with the given pa-
==PAGE:65==
4.4 Hard EDF approach
55
rameters. The ... represent much of the same code as FIFO and the complete
function can be seen in Appendix H.

One big diﬀerence between FIFO and EDF scheduling is that EDF should be
able to move appointments. EDF uses the same function as FIFO, getAvailable
TreatmentIntervals, to get available intervals though when appointments can be
moved, this function calls the function availablePrioritizedTreatmentIntervals
which is shown in Listing 4.9. Figure 4.4 shows the ﬂow of the function, where
the function ﬁrst tries to ﬁnd intervals within the deadline without moving any
appointments. If this is not possible then the function increments the fromDate
with the minimum days given, before moving appointments and starts to search
for intervals. The next search also collects appointments that lie in the intervals
found, such that when iterating the intervals, the correct appointments can be
moved. As mentioned in Section 3.3.5, the hard EDF scheduling should fail if it
cannot ﬁnd an appointment within a deadline, as the deadlines cannot be held.
This is demonstrated in Figure 4.4, where the crossed state should be viewed as
a failed state.
The function availablePrioritizedTreatmentIntervals takes the same param-
eters as availableTreatmentIntervals but also uses the iteration count, to keep
track of the depth of moving patients. As it can be seen in the listing, the
functions calls availableTreatmentIntervals without prioritization ﬁrst, which
if this results in intervals then returns. If not, the from date is updated and
prioritized intervals are passed along to the nested function internalFunc. The
internal function does, based on the conﬁguration option getAllInt, iterate over

4.5
Discussion
Through the implementation, the approach has been to make the code develop-
ment as general as possible. Future scheduling methods might be adopted, so
implementing functions that can be used by all types of scheduling methods can
beneﬁt the implementation done for other scheduling methods. Some beneﬁts
and drawbacks arise when implementing in general, and these will be discussed
in this section.
4.5.1
Combinations
From Section 3.1.5, all treatments can include a set of personal and equipment
resources that should be used.
This combination can be critical for looking
up intervals, as with higher resource demand, the larger the search must be
==PAGE:68==
58
Implementation
for ﬁnding an available appointment. This arises when the hospital calendar
construction in Section 3.1.7 gives a schedule for each resource. Hence when
looking for an appointment for a set of resources, all schedules must be traversed
for each type of resource and the intersecting intervals between all schedules are
where appointments can be placed. To give a better explanation, examples will
be given.
Imagine an admission treatment type, where the requirements for an admission
is a bed, bed table and a heart rate monitor. If a ward department should be
able to house 30 admitted patients, the ward department needs 30 beds, 30 bed
tables and 30 heart rate monitors. Without the assumption that a bed must have
a speciﬁc bed table and a heart rate monitor combined with it, 27.000 (30·30·30)
combinations of resources can be possible for an admission treatment. To search
for a possible appointment and support the general approach, all combinations
between the beds, bed tables and heart rate monitors must be looked up, to see
if an appointment is possible.
Another example could be a surgery treatment type. These often have a anes-
thetist, surgeon, some nurses and a bed together with heart rate monitor. For
this example, a surgery only consist of 1 doctor, 2 nurses, 1 bed and 1 heart
rate monitor. Imagine a surgery department where there are 3 available sur-
geons, 6 nurses, 3 beds and 3 heart rate monitors available. This makes the
surgery department capable of handling 3 operations simultaneously. The com-
binations for this surgery department, can be calculated with the help of the
following formula, where n is the number of available resources and r is the
amount needed:
C(n, r) =
n!
(r! · (n −r)!)
This gives a value of 15 for the nurses, while a combination of nurses with
surgeons, beds and monitors, becomes a total of 405 (15·3·3·3). This might not
seem much, but as Sections 3.4.2.3 states, admission appointments are searched
for together with a surgery. Hence when looking for one of the 405 possible
surgery appointments, each 27.000 possible admission appointments have to be
compared with, giving a total of 12.150.000 (405 · 27000) combinations.
This problem of combinations was discovered through the implementation. To
handle this, the schedule traversing is only done once for each resource, so that
only intersections between intervals must be done 27000 times, for the admission
example above.
==PAGE:69==
4.5 Discussion
59
4.5.2
Dividing of intervals
Continuing the problem from Section 4.5.1, personal resource intervals are often
divided into days because of shifts. Assumption (3) from Section 3.4.1 does not
allow for shifts over days, so when extracting intervals for personal, the intervals
will always have a maximum duration of a day. On the contrary, equipment can
be used at any time, and because intervals can last over several days, interval
comparisons between equipment resources is much faster. The demonstrate the
problem of dividing intervals, the surgery example from Section 4.5.1 will be
extended.
It was found that 405 diﬀerent combinations of surgery appointments are pos-
sible. Imagine now that the look ahead limit for simulation is 180 days. As
available intervals for personal are divided into shifts on days, this becomes
72.900 (405 · 180) future intervals that should be collected. Together with the
admission intervals, the search becomes enormous and become even larger if the
interval found on a day is divided further. If an interval from 8-16 on a given
day is found, and a surgery takes 2 hours, there might not be an admission
appointment available at 10, but only at 11.
The optimization from Section 4.5.1 handles look up in schedules, so only inter-
sections are done, which is still fast. The problem arises further with surgery
and admission appointments, as there are many combinations. To cope with
this, searching for admission interval after each single surgery appointment, is
only done for the days that the admission lasts. Furthermore, every appoint-
ment tried on a speciﬁc date and time is added to a dictionary. So when the
same appointment is possible, but with another combination of personal and
equipment, it is not tried.
4.5.3
First available appointment
When simulating, the ﬁrst available appointment is always chosen.
Neither
FIFO of EDF scheduling places appointments randomly in the future, so this
was not needed. In reality though, appointments made with patients, is often
done with the same doctor.
==PAGE:70==
60
Implementation
4.5.4
Appointments anywhere
A huge beneﬁt of the general implementation is that all future possible appoint-
ments are found, making it possible to place appointments anywhere in the
future. While this is not being used with FIFO or hard EDF simulation, it can
be that other scheduling methods want to place appointments randomly in the
future. A scheduling method adopted in [14], reserves time slots for priority pa-
tients. Hence future dates are needed to be searched for appointments, making
the general implementation a beneﬁt for this.
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 5
Result and Analysis
In this chapter, the results of simulating diﬀerent scheduling methods with dif-
ferent parameters will be presented. Analyzing factors will be listed to be able
to analyze and compare the diﬀerent simulations with each other. All simula-
tions presented in this chapter, simulated a total of 180 days from 1/1-19 to
30/6-19.
5.1
Factors
To be able to see how good a scheduling method works for the hospital system,
certain types of factors should be looked upon. In the comparison, one schedul-
ing method might give a better result for the patients without prioritization
while another gives the better result for patients with prioritization. The pur-
pose of this thesis is not to choose a speciﬁc scheduling method that should be
used, but to make a comparison and allow for the reader to select a scheduling
that ﬁts most needs of the hospital system. Hence the following factors will
show how well the scheduling method performs for the hospital system.
==PAGE:72==
62
Result and Analysis
5.1.1
Deadlines missed
On very relevant factor for Region Sjælland and the whole Danish health care
sector is the ability to meet deadlines on the cancer packages. So one important
factor to look on is how good the scheduling method ability is to meet deadlines.
To measure this factor, patients with deadlines must be part of the simulation,
and then the number of patients who misses deadlines on any treatment will be
accounted for.
Listing 5.1 shows the functions which can be used, to analyze and get the jour-
nals of patients, who have either missed their deadlines, or where patients have
not got an appointment, before the lookahead limit. The later one allows for
testing how low, a lookahead can be before it is not met.

that the hospital system has. One way to measure this, is by deﬁning a range of

is used:

5.1.3
Costs
Costs is another factor to use, as the more resources the hospital system needs,
the more money is has to spent. Some resources, like personal, have costs on a
monthly basis while equipment resources is more often a one cost only, unless
the resource is leased. The cost is closely related to the resource utilization as
a higher cost might be justiﬁable my a high utilization while a medium cost
can be justiﬁable by a utilization factor between 0.7 and 0.8. No functions have
been implemented to analyze the costs as the data used is ﬁctional.
==PAGE:74==
64
Result and Analysis
5.1.4
Queue stacking
Queue stacking is a relevant factor to show how patients must wait longer to get
an appointment. If there are not enough resources, the queue will automatically
stack up, why the contrary would be if there are to many resources, the queue
will never stack up. Hence this factor will help ﬁnding a balance between the
two, such that a fair amount of queue is possible with the lowest amount of
resources, keeping the costs down. An .xlsx (Microsoft Excel) ﬁle is generated
with queue stack for every resource, on every date, to help analyzing the result.
Listing 5.3 shows the function for generating the ﬁle. This function uses the
NuGet package EPPlus to generate Excel documents.

5.2
Simulation parameters
To get any result at all, a given set of parameters must be deﬁned to be able to
simulate. Since the hospital model described in Section 3.1 is very broad, and
all parameters are adjustable, some parameters must be restricted, for getting a
result at all. For this speciﬁc result and analyze, the following parameters have
been ﬁxed:
Hospital system
Table 5.1 shows the hospital structure that has been used for simulation. Only
one physical hospital has been used, which then has 8 departments. 7 (100-700)
departments can be considered as departments that focus on a speciﬁc part
of the medical science. The last department, 800, focuses on scanning patients
with for example a CT-scanner. To support the designed model, all departments
are separated into sub-departments where each sub-department has their own
focus.
Hospital calendar
As stated in Section 3.1.7, the schedules include dates for when resources are
available. This could introduce days when equipment is not available, for ex-
ample when equipment is broken or scheduled for yearly inspection. For the

purpose of simplifying the simulation, this has not been used, so the assump-
tion is made that equipment is always available. Personal is restricted by shifts,
which is described later.
Patient processes
Two types of patient processes have been used to simulate patients with and
without deadlines, which can be seen in Appendix B. The two patient processes
are exactly the same, in terms of treatments, where the diﬀerence being that one
patient process has deadlines on treatments (to imitate a cancer package). These
patient processes have been created for all 7 departments, while department 800
is used as part of the patient process (to imitate a shared resource between all
departments).
Patient arrivals
With the two types of patient processes deﬁned, their arrival rate must also
be deﬁned.
To simplify the simulation, only one scenario of patient arrivals
have been used. This scenario should be seen as the average number of patients
arriving at each department every day.
Hence the following values are the
lambda values given to the Poisson distribution for each patient process:
• Patient process 1: 7
==PAGE:76==
66
Result and Analysis
• Patient process 2: 2
Minimum days before moving, prioritized can move prioritized and
depth limits
As mentioned in Section 3.3.5, multiple parameters can be given to EDF when
prioritizing patients.
These have also been been ﬁxed to the following, for
simpliﬁcation:
• Minimum days before moving: 2
• Prioritized can moved prioritized: false
• Moving patients depth limit: 1
The above restrictions means that when getting prioritized appointments, there
must go 2 days of the current day before a patient can be moved. This seems as
a fair restriction, so patients who have an appointment the same day and day
after cannot get their appointment moved. As the depth for moving patients is
1, meaning that if a prioritized is trying to move another prioritized, it will fail,
then it does not make sense to allow it. The depth if restricted to 1 to simplify
the simulation.
Other parameters
The other parameters that can be given the simulation is the number of personal
and equipment resources, a shift calendar and reasonable look ahead limit (also
known as maximum waiting days). These parameters will be adjusted to reach
diﬀerent goals of the simulation and to show diﬀerent outcomes.
5.3
Simulation Result
In the upcoming simulations, patients without deadlines had an average arrival
rate of 7 and patients with deadline had 2. With this being set on all 7 de-
partments, gives an average rate 63 patients arriving every day with 9 being
the average in each department. The following will show diﬀerent attempts of
simulator parameters. All the attempts will be scheduled with FIFO ﬁrst, and
then if an attempt seems ﬁt for an EDF scheduling, meaning that deadlines
have not been met, the attempt will be tried with EDF scheduling to see if it
helps and what the consequences are.
==PAGE:77==
5.3 Simulation Result
67
5.3.1
Simulation 1
From the patient processes described in Section 5.2, the longest consultation that
can happen, lasts for 45 minutes. A surgery takes 120 min, an admission 3 days
and a scanning examination takes 15 minutes. To cope with these duration’s,
the following is a suggestion to having enough resources:
• Consultation and admission department: 2 doctors and 36 beds, bed tables
and monitors.
• Surgery department: 3 doctors, 6 nurses, 3 beds and 3 monitors.
• Scanner department: 3 radiographers and 3 scanners.
• All shifts of personal are from 8-16 all days of the week (including week-
ends)
The above resource dimensions gives a total of 16 hours a day for consulta-
tion, 24 hours of operations, and 24 hours of scans. The 36 beds allows for
36 patients to be admitted at the same time. These resource dimensions ﬁt
well with the above duration of treatments and should allow for all patients to
not miss a deadline nor having queues stacking up. The result of running the
function getPatientsWithTreatmentDeadlinesMissed in Listing 5.1 showed that
no patients missed their treatment deadlines. Table 5.2 shows the resource uti-
lization for this simulation. Here it is worth noticing that equipment utilization
is very low in surgery and scanning. That is because the equipment is available
24/7, but personal is not, so the utilization for equipment becomes lower than
the personal. The other departments with another part of the medical science
are not shown in Table 5.2 as their are very similar to the result of the ﬁrst
department.

ulation.
5.3.2
Simulation 2
From Section 5.3.1, it was shown that utilization of admission equipment is not
optimal. What is tried in this simulation is lowering the possible of admitted
patients to an amount of 26. The other resources are kept intact. Table 5.3
shows that the admission utilization increases, while the surgery personal de-
creases little. Even though the amount of possible admitted patients is lowered,
deadlines are still met for all patients. The costs are lowered comparing with
Simulation 1 and the queue stack does not show longer queues, but it does show
that all beds are used at some point.

miss their deadline. As Figure 5.2 shows, the queue never balances, which also
gives the reason for missing deadlines.

Simulation 4

the number of possible admitted patients was risen to 26 again, but all weekend
shifts were changed to 8-12 in one run and 8-10 in another run. The result is

5.3.5
Simulation 5
In simulation 4, deadlines were missed with the weekend shifts being 8-10. Run-
ning EDF with these parameters shows a halt at 27/6, where the error being
that no possible prioritized appointment can be found from 27/6 - 30/6. This
comes from the examination treatment after surgery, where the deadline is 3
days. What happens is, that all appointments in the scanner department, are
ﬁlled with priority patients, so when another priority patient is trying to get an
appointment, it fails, as it cannot move the other ones.
With the utilization factor on admission equipment only increasing by 0,01,
compared to the result from FIFO, the utilization factors will not be compared.
Thought testing the look ahead limit shows something interesting, using the
function in Section 5.1.1. Table 5.6 shows, that when simulating with FIFO,
a guaranty can be made for 11 days for getting an appointment. With EDF,
the guaranty is increased to 23. Furthermore, the Patients columns show the
amount of patients that missed the look ahead limit found by FIFO. If 10 were
to be used as the lookahead limit, 31 patients in FIFO could not be guarantied
an appointment while the amount being 120 for EDF.

weekend shift was 8-12 for the scanning department. To test this, the shift is
set to 8-11. Table 5.7 shows the utilization result, where surgery utilization has
increased compared with the other simulations. All deadlines were met in this
simulation, so 1 more hour availability in scanning can make a diﬀerence.

==PAGE:83==
Chapter 6
Conclusion
6.1
Summary
This thesis began with describing the modern hospital system and by intro-
ducing the problems that the hospital system faces in current time but also in
the future. With increasing population and the average living age rising, more
people face being sick with diseases that need treatment by a hospital system.
Hospital systems have always had ﬁnite resources, and will continue to have it
in the future. So making optimization and clever scheduling of patients is ever
more needed.
To help with the problems that modern hospital systems face, the fundamentals
of the hospital system was ﬁrst presented together with an analogy between the
hospital- and multiprocessor system. This was done to be able to beneﬁt from
the scheduling methods that already exist in the multiprocessor system.
A design of the hospital system model, derived from the fundamentals of the
hospital, was described such that scheduling methods could use it. The schedul-
ing methods FIFO and EDF seemed most ﬁt with the hospital system, while
SJF and FPS would not result in the kind of scheduling a general hospital sys-
tem needs. FIFO and EDF was then chosen for further development, and was
to be used in the latter simulation.
==PAGE:84==
74
Conclusion
An implementation of the simulation tool, to simulate the chosen scheduling
methods was then presented. Here the general important functions were outlined
together with the functions for simulating FIFO and EDF. The implementation
showed that many optimization problems arises when dealing with a large and
complex system, as the hospital system is.
The latter result and analysis showed that many factors are present when com-
paring scheduling methods. Diﬀerent simulations were presented where even
small adjustments to the parameters, were shown to a have an impact on the
result.
6.2
Objectives Fulﬁllment
The objectives for this thesis were the following:
1. Compare the hospital system with the heterogeneous multiprocessor sys-
tem.
2. Model the hospital system.
3. Apply scheduling methods from a heterogeneous multiprocessor system to
the hospital system.
4. Analyze the result.
Objective (1) was done in Chapter 2 and 3 where the result was that the two
systems seem ﬁt for a comparison, with certain abstractions. When scheduling
in a multiprocessor system, it is done by the multiprocessor as a master, meaning
that the multiprocessor receives the task and then evaluates for which processor
the task must be sent to. While in the hospital system, it is the departments who
do the scheduling themselves, and the hospital only handles shared resources.
The comparison made in the above chapters showed, that the hospital is the
multiprocessor and the departments are processors so the scheduling is done
on diﬀerent levels in each system. Though, this is an abstraction that can be
accepted, as the whole systems are comparable in terms of operating units and
shared resources.
Chapter 3 fulﬁlled objective (2), as the a model of the hospital system was pre-
sented as types in the F# programming language. Objective (3) was completed
in Chapter 3 and 4, where a simulator for the scheduling method FIFO and
another simulator for the scheduling method EDF was developed.
==PAGE:85==
6.3 Future Development
75
The two scheduling methods used laid the foundation for objective (4), which
in Chapter 5 was presented. Here certain factors to analyze was given together
with parameters that should be used in simulation. Running simulations showed
that there are beneﬁts of adjusting diﬀerent parameters.
6.3
Future Development
Many paths can be taken for the future development of this simulation tool.
One very interesting path to take is to gather a part of a realistic resource map
in a current hospital system together with statistics over patient arrivals. The
reason for the "partly" is, that the entire hospital system is huge, and modelling
every patient process possible seems impractical. Without ending up like other
optimization done on a single departments, one would have to take a bundle of
departments that also use shared resources, like the test data created for this
thesis.
To extend to usage of real data above, it is possible to extend the simulation
with automatic testing. Evaluation would have to be done on a the result of
a simulation, with a given set of parameters, and then a new prediction of
parameters, that might give a better result could then be used in the simulator.
The tests done in this thesis are manually, which is not very eﬃcient.
Another path could lead to the usage of costs on the edges for patient processes.
The patient process tree in Section 3.1.5 is a great candidate for this, where
costs of going speciﬁc paths in the tree could be calculated to see what the costs
of simulations are.
Natural extensions for the simulation include looking into other scheduling meth-
ods used in the multiprocessor system. One scheduling method that could be
used, though without looking to much into it, is a server based scheduling. This
scheduling has servers which in theory consist of periodic tasks, whose purpose
is to server aperiodic tasks [6]. This type of service could be compared with a
blood sampling unit in a hospital, where nurses take blood samples from pa-
tients for the departments. Other scheduling methods could include evaluation
on the queue stack being generated. With an evaluation doing simulation, extra
resources could be applied if need while resources can be removed, or placed
elsewhere, if queues do not stack up.
Using soft EDF scheduling could also be applied together with urgent patients,
such that an ER could be simulated, where patients arrive without a reference
from their general practitioner. This type of scheduling might require that the
==PAGE:86==
76
Conclusion
shift plan is not strictly bound, as it is in the developed simulator.
In terms of software, a better EDF implementation could be made to support all
the cases there are for moving appointments around. In other software paths,
one could use parallelism to optimize the speed of the simulations. This would
though require in heavy semaphore usage, as it should be possible to book an
appointment twice.
6.4
Conclusion
The analogy presented in this thesis works well for the hospital system. The
scheduling methods applied, mostly apply to single processors, which might be
the case why they work so good with the hospital system, as each department
can be seen as a processor. The handling of shared resources between entities is
a great puzzle in hospital- and multiprocessor system, and the analogy helps to
see solutions for this. Processes and patient processes work well together with
tasks and treatments.
So to use scheduling methods from computer science
seems ﬁt for scheduling in hospital systems.
Flaws were unfortunately found late in the implementation, which made some
EDF simulations impossible to make. Diﬀerent corner cases of scheduling ap-
pointments were not tested properly, so when simulating, the ﬂaws popped up
and had to be ﬁxed. FIFO on the other hand, works ﬂawless and is able to
schedule with any given parameters.
Lastly, all the simulations made, with the diﬀerent parameters showed how big
of a puzzle it is to estimate a hospitals needs. There are many parameters, and
there might be a reason for why no one has taken the challenge of simulating a
complete hospital. With this in mind though, a good model has been presented
to handle the all the parameters and serves well for future development.

let mapTreatmentTypeToJournalTreatmentType (origT:Treatment)
(pIds:Set<Id>) (eIds:Set<Id>)
399
(originalDate:Date) (fromDate:Time ∗Date) (toDate:Time ∗
Date) (isAdmissionEnd:bool) (appHist:AppointmentHistory) =
400
let (Node(tt,_,_,_)) = origT
401
match tt with
402
| Consultation (desc, depId,_,_,_,_) −>
JournalConsultation(desc, depId, pIds, eIds, originalDate,
fromDate,
403
toDate, "", appHist, origT)
404
| Examination (desc, depId,_,_,_,_) −>
JournalExamination(desc, depId, pIds, eIds, originalDate, fromDate,
405
toDate, "", appHist, origT)
406
| Surgery (desc, depId,_,_,_,_) −> JournalSurgery(desc, depId,
pIds, eIds, originalDate, fromDate, toDate, "",
407
appHist,
origT)
408
| Admission (desc, depId,_,_) −>

411

originalDate, fromDate, "", appHist, origT)
412
| Entrance −> failwithf "Cannot map Entrace to
JournalTreatmentType!"

| Exit −> JournalTreatmentExit("Treatment ended")
